<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<p><b>Marlon Brando, Jr.</b> (April 3, 1924 – July 1, 2004) was an American actor, film director and activist. He is credited with bringing realism to film acting, and is considered one of the greatest and most influential actors of all time. He helped to popularize the <a href="/wiki/Stanislavski%27s_system" title="Stanislavski's system">Stanislavski system of acting</a>, studying with <a href="/wiki/Stella_Adler" title="Stella Adler">Stella Adler</a> in the 1940s. Brando is most famous for his <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a>-winning performances as Terry Malloy in <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i> (1954) and <a href="/wiki/Vito_Corleone" title="Vito Corleone">Vito Corleone</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">The Godfather</a></i> (1972), as well as performances in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1951_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1951 film)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i> (1951), <i><a href="/wiki/Viva_Zapata!" title="Viva Zapata!">Viva Zapata!</a></i> (1952), <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1953_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1953 film)">Julius Caesar</a></i> (1953), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild_One" title="The Wild One">The Wild One</a></i> (1953), <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_in_a_Golden_Eye_(film)" title="Reflections in a Golden Eye (film)">Reflections in a Golden Eye</a></i> (1967), <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Tango_in_Paris" title="Last Tango in Paris">Last Tango in Paris</a></i> (1972), and <i><a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_Now" title="Apocalypse Now">Apocalypse Now</a></i> (1979). Brando was also an <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activist</a> for many causes, notably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American_Civil_Rights_Movement_(1954%E2%80%9368)" title="African-American Civil Rights Movement (1954–68)">African-American Civil Rights Movement</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> movements.</p>
<p>He initially gained acclaim and an Academy Award nomination for reprising the role of <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kowalski" title="Stanley Kowalski">Stanley Kowalski</a> in the 1951 film adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Williams" title="Tennessee Williams">Tennessee Williams</a>' play <i><a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire" title="A Streetcar Named Desire">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i>, a role that he had originated successfully on <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a>. He received further praise for his performance as Terry Malloy in <i>On the Waterfront</i>, and his portrayal of the rebel motorcycle gang leader Johnny Strabler in <i>The Wild One</i> proved to be a lasting image in popular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[3]</a></sup> Brando received Academy Award nominations for playing <a href="/wiki/Emiliano_Zapata" title="Emiliano Zapata">Emiliano Zapata</a> in <i>Viva Zapata!</i>; <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> in <a href="/wiki/Joseph_L._Mankiewicz" title="Joseph L. Mankiewicz">Joseph L. Mankiewicz</a>'s 1953 <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1953_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1953 film)">film adaptation</a> of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i>; and Air Force Major Lloyd Gruver in <i><a href="/wiki/Sayonara" title="Sayonara">Sayonara</a></i> (1957), an adaption of <a href="/wiki/James_A._Michener" title="James A. Michener">James Michener</a>'s 1954 novel. Brando was included in a list of <a href="/wiki/Top_Ten_Money_Making_Stars_Poll" title="Top Ten Money Making Stars Poll">Top Ten Money Making Stars</a> three times in the 1950s, coming in at number 10 in 1954, number 6 in 1955, and number 4 in 1958.</p>
<p>The 1960s proved to be a fallow decade for Brando. He directed and starred in the <a href="/wiki/Cult_film" title="Cult film">cult</a> western film <i><a href="/wiki/One-Eyed_Jacks" title="One-Eyed Jacks">One-Eyed Jacks</a></i>, a critical and commercial flop, after which he delivered a series of box-office failures, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty_(1962_film)" title="Mutiny on the Bounty (1962 film)">1962 film adaptation</a> of the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty_(novel)" title="Mutiny on the Bounty (novel)">Mutiny on the Bounty</a></i>. After 10 years, during which he did not appear in a successful film, he won his second Academy Award for playing Vito Corleone in <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">Francis Ford Coppola</a>'s <i>The Godfather</i>, a role critics consider among his greatest. <i>The Godfather</i> was then one of the most commercially successful films of all time. With that and his Oscar-nominated performance in <i>Last Tango in Paris</i>, Brando re-established himself in the ranks of top box-office stars, placing sixth and tenth in the Money Making Stars poll in 1972 and 1973, respectively. Brando took a four-year hiatus before appearing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Missouri_Breaks" title="The Missouri Breaks">The Missouri Breaks</a></i> (1976). After this, he was content with being a highly paid <a href="/wiki/Character_actor" title="Character actor">character actor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">cameo roles</a>, such as in <i><a href="/wiki/Superman_(1978_film)" title="Superman (1978 film)">Superman</a></i> (1978) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Formula_(1980_film)" title="The Formula (1980 film)">The Formula</a></i> (1980), before taking a nine-year break from motion pictures. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a></i>, Brando was paid a record $3.7 million ($15 million in inflation-adjusted dollars) and 11.75% of the gross profits for 13 days' work on <i>Superman</i>. He finished out the 1970s with his controversial performance as <a href="/wiki/Colonel_Kurtz" title="Colonel Kurtz">Colonel Kurtz</a> in another Coppola film, <i>Apocalypse Now</i>, a box-office hit for which he was highly paid and which helped finance his career layoff during the 1980s.</p>
<p>Brando was ranked by the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> as the <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Stars" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Stars">fourth-greatest movie star</a> among male movie stars whose screen debuts occurred in or before 1950. He was one of only three professional actors, along with <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a>, named in 1999 by <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine as one of its <a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">100 Most Important People of the Century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup> He died of <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a> on July 1, 2004, at age 80.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Personal_life"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Personal life</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brando was born on April 3, 1924, in <a href="/wiki/Omaha,_Nebraska" title="Omaha, Nebraska">Omaha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, to Marlon Brando, Sr. (1895–1965), a pesticide and chemical feed manufacturer, and Dorothy Julia (née Pennebaker; 1897–1954).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-7">[5]</a></sup> Brando had two older sisters, <a href="/wiki/Jocelyn_Brando" title="Jocelyn Brando">Jocelyn Brando</a> (1919–2005) and Frances (1922–1994). His ancestry included German, Dutch, English, and Irish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-songs_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-songs-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> His patrilineal immigrant ancestor, Johann Wilhelm Brandau, arrived in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> in the early 1700s from the Palatinate of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rand12_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-rand12-11">[9]</a></sup> Brando was raised a <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Scientist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> His mother, known as Dodie, was unconventional for her time; an actress herself, she smoked, wore trousers and drove cars—all unusual for women at the time—and was even a theatre administrator, helping <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">Henry Fonda</a> begin his acting career. However, she was an alcoholic and often had to be brought home from <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> bars by her husband. In his autobiography, <i>Songs My Mother Taught Me</i>, Brando expressed sadness when writing about his mother: "The anguish that her drinking produced was that she preferred getting drunk to caring for us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> Dodie and Brando's father eventually joined <a href="/wiki/Alcoholics_Anonymous" title="Alcoholics Anonymous">Alcoholics Anonymous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> Brando harbored far more enmity for his father, stating, "I was his namesake, but nothing I did ever pleased or even interested him. He enjoyed telling me I couldn't do anything right. He had a habit of telling me I would never amount to anything."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> Brando's parents moved to <a href="/wiki/Evanston,_Illinois" title="Evanston, Illinois">Evanston, Illinois</a>, when his father's work took him to <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, but separated when Brando was 11 years old. His mother took the three children to <a href="/wiki/Santa_Ana,_California" title="Santa Ana, California">Santa Ana, California</a>, where they lived with her mother. In 1937, Brando's parents reconciled and moved together to <a href="/wiki/Libertyville,_Illinois" title="Libertyville, Illinois">Libertyville, Illinois</a>, a small town north of Chicago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> In 1939 and 1941, he worked as an usher at the town's only movie theatre, The Liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forrest1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forrest1-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando, whose childhood nickname was "Bud", was a mimic from his youth. He developed an ability to absorb the mannerisms of kids he played with and display them dramatically while staying in character. He was introduced to neighborhood boy <a href="/wiki/Wally_Cox" title="Wally Cox">Wally Cox</a> and the two were unlikely closest friends until Cox' death in 1973. In the 2007 TCM biopic, <i>Brando: The Documentary</i>, childhood friend <a href="/wiki/George_Englund" title="George Englund">George Englund</a> recalls Brando's earliest acting as imitating the cows and horses on the family farm as a way to distract his mother from drinking. His sister Jocelyn was the first to pursue an acting career, going to study at the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Dramatic_Arts" title="American Academy of Dramatic Arts">American Academy of Dramatic Arts</a> in New York City. She appeared on Broadway, then films and television. Brando's sister Frances left college in California to study art in New York. Brando had been held back a year in school and was later expelled from <a href="/wiki/Libertyville_High_School" title="Libertyville High School">Libertyville High School</a> for riding his motorcycle through the corridors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was sent to <a href="/wiki/Shattuck-Saint_Mary%27s" title="Shattuck-Saint Mary's">Shattuck Military Academy</a>, where his father had studied before him. Brando excelled at theatre and did well in the school. In his final year (1943), he was put on probation for being insubordinate to a visiting army colonel during maneuvers. He was confined to his room, but sneaked into town and was caught. The faculty voted to expel him, though he was supported by the students, who thought expulsion was too harsh. He was invited back for the following year, but decided instead to drop out of high school. Brando worked as a ditch-digger as a summer job arranged by his father. He tried to enlist in the Army, but his induction physical revealed that a football injury he had sustained at Shattuck had left him with a <a href="/wiki/Luxating_patella" title="Luxating patella">trick knee</a>. He was classified <a href="/wiki/Selective_Service_System" title="Selective Service System">4-F</a> and not inducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-songs_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-songs-8">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_and_acting">New York and acting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: New York and acting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brando decided to follow his sisters to New York, studying at the American Theatre Wing Professional School, part of the Dramatic Workshop of the <a href="/wiki/The_New_School" title="The New School">New School</a>, with influential German director <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Piscator" title="Erwin Piscator">Erwin Piscator</a>. In a 1988 documentary, <i>Marlon Brando: The Wild One</i>, Brando's sister Jocelyn remembered, "He was in a school play and enjoyed it ... So he decided he would go to New York and study acting because that was the only thing he had enjoyed. That was when he was 18." In the A&amp;E <i>Biography</i> episode on Brando, George Englund said Brando fell into acting in New York because "he was accepted there. He wasn't criticized. It was the first time in his life that he heard good things about himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando was an avid student and proponent of <a href="/wiki/Stella_Adler" title="Stella Adler">Stella Adler</a>, from whom he learned the techniques of the <a href="/wiki/Stanislavski%27s_system" title="Stanislavski's system">Stanislavski system</a>. This technique encouraged the actor to explore his own feelings and past experiences to fully realize the character being portrayed. Brando's remarkable insight and sense of realism were evident early on. Adler used to recount that when teaching Brando, she had instructed the class to act like chickens, and added that a nuclear bomb was about to fall on them. Most of the class clucked and ran around wildly, but Brando sat calmly and pretended to lay an egg. Asked by Adler why he had chosen to react this way, he said, "I'm a chicken—what do I know about bombs?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> Despite being commonly regarded as a Method actor, Brando disagreed. He claimed to have abhorred <a href="/wiki/Lee_Strasberg" title="Lee Strasberg">Lee Strasberg</a>'s teachings:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>After I had some success, Lee Strasberg tried to take credit for teaching me how to act. He never taught me anything. He would have claimed credit for the sun and the moon if he believed he could get away with it. He was an ambitious, selfish man who exploited the people who attended the Actors Studio and tried to project himself as an acting oracle and guru. Some people worshipped him, but I never knew why. I sometimes went to the Actors Studio on Saturday mornings because <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">Elia Kazan</a> was teaching, and there were usually a lot of good-looking girls, but Strasberg never taught me acting. <a href="/wiki/Stella_Adler" title="Stella Adler">Stella (Adler)</a> did—and later Kazan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career:_1944.E2.80.931951">Early career: 1944–1951</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early career: 1944–1951">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3757" data-file-width="2681" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg/220px-Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg/330px-Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg/440px-Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brando_van_Vechten3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 24-year-old Marlon Brando on the set of the Broadway production of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i>, 1948.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brando used his Stanislavski System skills for his first <a href="/wiki/Summer_stock_theatre" title="Summer stock theatre">summer stock</a> roles in <a href="/wiki/Sayville,_New_York" title="Sayville, New York">Sayville, New York</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>. Brando established a pattern of erratic, insubordinate behavior in the few shows he had been in. His behavior had him kicked out of the cast of the New School's production in Sayville, but he was soon afterwards discovered in a locally produced play there. Then, in 1944, he made it to <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> in the bittersweet drama <i><a href="/wiki/I_Remember_Mama_(play)" title="I Remember Mama (play)">I Remember Mama</a></i>, playing the son of <a href="/wiki/Mady_Christians" title="Mady Christians">Mady Christians</a>. The Lunts wanted Brando to play the role of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Lunt" title="Alfred Lunt">Alfred Lunt</a>'s son in <i>O Mistress Mine</i>, and Lunt even coached him for his audition, but Brando's reading during the audition was so desultory that they couldn't hire him. <a href="/wiki/New_York_Drama_Critics_Award" title="New York Drama Critics Award">New York Drama Critics</a> voted him "Most Promising Young Actor" for his role as an anguished veteran in <i><a href="/wiki/Truckline_Cafe" title="Truckline Cafe">Truckline Café</a></i>, although the play was a commercial failure. In 1946, he appeared on Broadway as the young hero in the political drama <i><a href="/wiki/A_Flag_is_Born" title="A Flag is Born">A Flag is Born</a></i>, refusing to accept wages above the <a href="/wiki/Actors%27_Equity_Association" title="Actors' Equity Association">Actors' Equity</a> rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journal_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-journal-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> In that same year, Brando played the role of Marchbanks alongside <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Cornell" title="Katharine Cornell">Katharine Cornell</a> in her production's revival of <i><a href="/wiki/Candida_(play)" title="Candida (play)">Candida</a></i>, one of her signature roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> Cornell also cast him as the Messenger in her production of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh">Jean Anouilh</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Antigone" title="Antigone">Antigone</a></i> that same year. He was also offered the opportunity to portray one of the principal characters in the Broadway premiere of <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O'Neill">Eugene O'Neill</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Iceman_Cometh" title="The Iceman Cometh">The Iceman Cometh</a></i>, but turned the part down after falling asleep while trying to read the massive script and pronouncing the play "ineptly written and poorly constructed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanfer2011a_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kanfer2011a-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1945, Brando's agent recommended he take a co-starring role in <i>The Eagle Has Two Heads</i> with <a href="/wiki/Tallulah_Bankhead" title="Tallulah Bankhead">Tallulah Bankhead</a>, produced by Jack Wilson. Tallulah had turned down the role of Blanche Dubois in <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i>, which Williams had written for her, to tour the play for the 1946–1947 season. Bankhead recognized Brando's potential, despite her disdain (which most Broadway veterans shared) for method acting, and agreed to hire him even though he auditioned poorly. The two clashed greatly during the pre-Broadway tour, with Bankhead reminding Brando of his mother, being her age and also having a drinking problem. Jack Wilson was largely tolerant of Brando's behavior, but he reached his limit when Brando mumbled through a dress rehearsal shortly before the November 28, 1946, opening. "I don't care what your grandmother did," Wilson exclaimed, "and that Method stuff, I want to know what you're going to do!" Brando in turn raised his voice, and acted with great power and passion. "It was marvelous," a cast member recalled. "Everybody hugged him and kissed him. He came ambling offstage and said to me, 'They don't think you can act unless you can yell.'" A review of Brando's performance in the opening assessed that Brando was "still building his character, but at present fails to impress".</p>
<p>One Boston critic remarked of Brando's prolonged death scene, "Brando looked like a car in midtown Manhattan searching for a parking space." He received better reviews at subsequent tour stops, but what his colleagues recalled was only occasional indications of the talent he would later demonstrate. "There were a few times when he was really magnificent," Tallulah admitted to an interviewer in 1962. "He was a great young actor when he wanted to be, but most of the time I couldn't even hear him on the stage." Brando displayed his apathy for the production by demonstrating some shocking onstage manners. He "tried everything in the world to ruin it for her", Tallulah's stage-manager claimed. "He nearly drove her crazy: scratching his crotch, picking his nose, doing anything." After several weeks on the road, they reached Boston, by which time Tallulah was ready to dismiss him. This proved to be one of the greatest blessings of his career, as it freed him up to play the role of <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kowalski" title="Stanley Kowalski">Stanley Kowalski</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Williams" title="Tennessee Williams">Tennessee Williams</a>'s 1947 play <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">Elia Kazan</a>. Bankhead had recommended him to Williams for the role of Stanley, thinking he was perfect for the part.</p>
<p>Pierpont writes that <a href="/wiki/John_Garfield" title="John Garfield">John Garfield</a> was first choice for the role, but "made impossible demands". It was Kazan's decision to fall back on the far less experienced (and technically too young for the role) Brando. In a letter dated August 29, 1947, Williams confided to his agent Audrey Wood: "It had not occurred to me before what an excellent value would come through casting a very young actor in this part. It humanizes the character of Stanley in that it becomes the brutality and callousness of youth rather than a vicious old man ... A new value came out of Brando's reading which was by far the best reading I have ever heard." Brando based his portrayal of Kowalski on the boxer <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Graziano" title="Rocky Graziano">Rocky Graziano</a>, whom he had studied at a local gymnasium. Graziano did not know who Brando was, but attended the production with tickets provided by the young man. He said, "The curtain went up and on the stage is that son of a bitch from the gym, and he's playing me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2013)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1947, Brando performed a screen test for an early <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Brothers</a> script for the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Rebel_Without_a_Cause" title="Rebel Without a Cause">Rebel Without a Cause</a></i> (1944), which bore no relation to the film eventually produced in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> The screen test is included as an extra in the 2006 DVD release of <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i>.</p>
<p>Brando's first screen role was the bitter paraplegic veteran in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Men_(film)" title="The Men (film)">The Men</a></i> (1950). He spent a month in bed at the Birmingham Army Hospital in <a href="/wiki/Van_Nuys" title="Van Nuys">Van Nuys</a> to prepare for the role. <i>The New York Times</i> reviewer <a href="/wiki/Bosley_Crowther" title="Bosley Crowther">Bosley Crowther</a> wrote that Brando as Ken "is so vividly real, dynamic and sensitive that his illusion is complete" and noted, "Out of stiff and frozen silences he can lash into a passionate rage with the tearful and flailing frenzy of a taut cable suddenly cut."</p>
<p>By Brando's own account, it may have been because of this film that his draft status was changed from 4-F to 1-A. He had had surgery on his trick knee, and it was no longer physically debilitating enough to incur exclusion from the draft. When Brando reported to the induction center, he answered a questionnaire by saying his <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">race</a> was "human", his color was "Seasonal–oyster white to beige", and he told an Army doctor that he was psychoneurotic. When the draft board referred him to a psychiatrist, Brando explained that he had been expelled from military school and had severe problems with authority. Coincidentally, the psychiatrist knew a doctor friend of Brando. Brando avoided military service during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-songs_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-songs-8">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early in his career, Brando began using <a href="/wiki/Cue_card" title="Cue card">cue cards</a> instead of memorizing his lines. Despite the objections of several of the film directors he worked with, Brando felt that this helped bring realism and spontaneity to his performances. He felt otherwise he would appear to be reciting a writer's speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> In the TV documentary <i>The Making of Superman: The Movie</i>, Brando explained:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If you don't know what the words are but you have a general idea of what they are, then you look at the cue card and it gives you the feeling to the viewer, hopefully, that the person is really searching for what he is going to say—that he doesn't know what to say.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, some thought Brando used the cards out of laziness or an inability to memorize his lines. Once on <i>The Godfather</i> set, Brando was asked why he wanted his lines printed out. He responded, "Because I can read them that way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_fame:_1951.E2.80.931954">Rise to fame: 1951–1954</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rise to fame: 1951–1954">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brando brought his performance as <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kowalski" title="Stanley Kowalski">Stanley Kowalski</a> to the screen in Tennessee William's <i><a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1951_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1951 film)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i> (1951). The role is regarded as one of Brando's greatest. The reception of Brando's performance was so positive that Brando quickly became a male sex symbol in Hollywood. The role earned him his first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> nomination in the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Best Actor category</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_(25).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="720" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg/220px-Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg/330px-Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg/440px-Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_%2825%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viva_Zapata_movie_trailer_screenshot_(25).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brando as <a href="/wiki/Emiliano_Zapata" title="Emiliano Zapata">Emiliano Zapata</a> in a trailer for the 1952 film <i>Viva Zapata!</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>He was also nominated the next year for <i><a href="/wiki/Viva_Zapata!" title="Viva Zapata!">Viva Zapata!</a></i> (1952), a fictionalized account of the life of Mexican revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Emiliano_Zapata" title="Emiliano Zapata">Emiliano Zapata</a>. It recounted his peasant upbringing, his rise to power in the early 20th century, and death. The film was directed by Elia Kazan and co-starred <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Quinn" title="Anthony Quinn">Anthony Quinn</a>. In the biopic <i>Marlon Brando: The Wild One</i>, Sam Shaw says, "Secretly, before the picture started, he went to Mexico to the very town where Zapata lived and was born in and it was there that he studied the speech patterns of people, their behavior, movement."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Most critics focused on the actor rather than the film, with <i>Time</i> and <i>Newsweek</i> publishing rave reviews.</p>
<p>Years later, in his autobiography, Brando remarked: "Tony Quinn, whom I admired professionally and liked personally, played my brother, but he was extremely cold to me while we shot that picture. During our scenes together, I sensed a bitterness toward me, and if I suggested a drink after work, he either turned me down or else was sullen and said little. Only years later did I learn why." Brando related that, to create on-screen tension between the two, "Gadg" (Kazan) had told Quinn—who had taken over the role of Stanley Kowalski on Broadway after Brando had finished—that Brando had been unimpressed with his work. After achieving the desired effect, Kazan never told Quinn that he had misled him. It was only many years later, after comparing notes, that Brando and Quinn realized the deception.</p>
<p>Brando's next film, <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1953_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1953 film)">Julius Caesar</a></i> (1953), received highly favorable reviews. Brando portrayed <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>. While most acknowledged Brando's talent, some critics felt Brando's "mumbling" and other idiosyncrasies betrayed a lack of acting fundamentals and, when his casting was announced, many remained dubious about his prospects for success. Directed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_L._Mankiewicz" title="Joseph L. Mankiewicz">Joseph L. Mankiewicz</a> and co-starring British stage actor <a href="/wiki/John_Gielgud" title="John Gielgud">John Gielgud</a>, Brando delivered an impressive performance, especially during Antony's noted "Friends, Romans, countrymen ..." speech. Gielgud was so impressed that he offered Brando a full season at the Hammersmith Theatre, an offer he declined. In his biography on the actor, Stefan Kanfer writes, "Marlon's autobiography devotes one line to his work on that film: Among all those British professionals, 'for me to walk onto a movie set and play Mark Anthony was asinine'—yet another example of his persistent self-denigration, and wholly incorrect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> Kanfer adds that after a screening of the film, director John Huston commented, "Christ! It was like a furnace door opening—the heat came off the screen. I don't know another actor who could do that."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the filming of <i>Julius Caesar</i>, Brando learned that Elia Kazan had cooperated with congressional investigators, naming a whole string of "subversives" to the <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Committee on Un-American Activities</a>. By all accounts, Brando was upset by his mentor's decision, but he worked with him again in <i>On The Waterfront</i>. "None of us is perfect," he later wrote in his memoir, "and I think that Gadg has done injury to others, but mostly to himself."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1953, Brando also starred in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wild_One" title="The Wild One">The Wild One</a></i>, riding his own <a href="/wiki/Triumph_Thunderbird" title="Triumph Thunderbird">Triumph Thunderbird</a> 6T motorcycle. Triumph's importers were ambivalent at the exposure, as the subject matter was rowdy motorcycle gangs taking over a small town. The film was criticized for its perceived gratuitous violence at the time, with <i>Time</i> stating, "The effect of the movie is not to throw light on the public problem, but to shoot adrenaline through the moviegoer's veins."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Brando allegedly did not see eye to eye with the Hungarian director <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3_Benedek" title="László Benedek">László Benedek</a> and did not get on with costar <a href="/wiki/Lee_Marvin" title="Lee Marvin">Lee Marvin</a>.</p>
<p>To Brando's expressed puzzlement, the movie inspired teen rebellion and made him a role model to the nascent rock-and-roll generation and future stars such as <a href="/wiki/James_Dean" title="James Dean">James Dean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>. After the movie's release, the sales of <a href="/wiki/Leather_jacket" title="Leather jacket">leather jackets</a> and blue <a href="/wiki/Jeans" title="Jeans">jeans</a> skyrocketed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Reflecting on the movie in his autobiography, Brando concluded that it had not aged very well but said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>More than most parts I've played in the movies or onstage, I related to Johnny, and because of this, I believe I played him as more sensitive and sympathetic than the script envisioned. There's a line in the picture where he snarls, 'Nobody tells me what to do.' That's exactly how I've felt all my life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (February 2014)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Later that same year, Brando starred in <a href="/wiki/Lee_Falk" title="Lee Falk">Lee Falk</a>'s production of <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Arms_and_the_Man" title="Arms and the Man">Arms and the Man</a></i> in Boston. Falk was proud to tell people that Brando turned down an offer of $10,000 per week on Broadway, in favor of working in his production in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, for less than $500 per week.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It was the last time Brando acted in a stage play.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_Waterfront"><i>On the Waterfront</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: On the Waterfront">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1954, Brando starred in <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i>, a crime drama film about union violence and corruption among longshoremen. The film was directed by Elia Kazan and written by <a href="/wiki/Budd_Schulberg" title="Budd Schulberg">Budd Schulberg</a>; it also stars <a href="/wiki/Karl_Malden" title="Karl Malden">Karl Malden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_J._Cobb" title="Lee J. Cobb">Lee J. Cobb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rod_Steiger" title="Rod Steiger">Rod Steiger</a> and, in her film debut, <a href="/wiki/Eva_Marie_Saint" title="Eva Marie Saint">Eva Marie Saint</a>. When initially offered the role, Brando—still stung by Kazan's testimony to HUAC—demurred and the part of Terry Malloy nearly went to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>. According to biographer Stefan Kanfer, the director believed that Sinatra, who grew up in <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">Hoboken</a>, would work as Malloy, but eventually producer <a href="/wiki/Sam_Spiegel" title="Sam Spiegel">Sam Spiegel</a> wooed Brando to the part, signing him for $100,000. "Kazan made no protest because, he subsequently confessed, 'I always preferred Brando to anybody.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanfer2011_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kanfer2011-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="695" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg/220px-Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg/330px-Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg/440px-Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eva_marie_saint_marlon_brando_waterfront_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brando with <a href="/wiki/Eva_Marie_Saint" title="Eva Marie Saint">Eva Marie Saint</a> in the trailer for <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i> (1954).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brando won the Oscar for his role as Terry Malloy in <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i>. His performance, spurred on by his rapport with Eva Marie Saint and Kazan's direction, was praised as a <i>tour de force</i>. For the famous <i>I coulda been a contender</i> scene, he convinced Kazan that the scripted scene was unrealistic. Schulberg's script had Brando acting the entire scene with his character being held at gunpoint by his brother Charlie, played by <a href="/wiki/Rod_Steiger" title="Rod Steiger">Rod Steiger</a>. Brando insisted on gently pushing away the gun, saying that Terry would never believe that his brother would pull the trigger and doubting that he could continue his speech while fearing a gun on him. Kazan let Brando improvise and later expressed deep admiration for Brando's instinctive understanding, saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>what was extraordinary about his performance, I feel, is the contrast of the tough-guy front and the extreme delicacy and gentle cast of his behavior. What other actor, when his brother draws a pistol to force him to do something shameful, would put his hand on the gun and push it away with the gentleness of a caress? Who else could read "Oh, Charlie!" in a tone of reproach that is so loving and so melancholy and suggests the terrific depth of pain? ... If there is a better performance by a man in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_film" title="History of film">history of film</a> in America, I don't know what it is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Girgus1998_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Girgus1998-33">[31]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Upon its release, <i>On the Waterfront</i> received glowing reviews from critics and was a commercial success, earning an estimated $4.2 million in rentals at the <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> box office in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> In his July 29, 1954, review, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> critic A. H. Weiler praised the film, calling it "an uncommonly powerful, exciting, and imaginative use of the screen by gifted professionals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> Film critic <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> lauded the film, stating that Brando and Kazan changed acting in American films forever and added it to his "Great Movies" list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> In his autobiography, Brando was typically dismissive of his performance: "On the day Gadg showed me the complete picture, I was so depressed by my performance I got up and left the screening room ... I thought I was a huge failure."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After Brando won the Academy Award for Best Actor, the statue was stolen. Much later, it turned up at a London auction house, which contacted the actor and informed him of its whereabouts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box_office_successes_and_directorial_debut:_1954.E2.80.931959">Box office successes and directorial debut: 1954–1959</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Box office successes and directorial debut: 1954–1959">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brando's first six films—<i>The Men</i>, <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i>, <i>Viva Zapata!</i>, <i>Julius Caesar</i>, <i>The Wild One</i> and <i>On the Waterfront</i>—laid a standard of excellence that would sustain him throughout his career—a standard that Brando himself would have difficulty reattaining. As the decade continued, Brando remained a top box office draw but critics felt his performances were half-hearted, lacking the intensity and commitment found in his earlier work, especially in his work with Kazan. He portrayed <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> in the 1954 film <i><a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9sir%C3%A9e_(film)" title="Désirée (film)">Désirée</a></i>. According to co-star <a href="/wiki/Jean_Simmons" title="Jean Simmons">Jean Simmons</a>, Brando's contract forced him to star in the movie. He put little effort into the role, claiming he didn't like the script, and later dismissed the entire movie as "superficial and dismal". Brando was especially contemptuous of director <a href="/wiki/Henry_Koster" title="Henry Koster">Henry Koster</a>.</p>
<p>Brando and Simmons were paired together again in the film adaptation of the musical <i><a href="/wiki/Guys_and_Dolls_(film)" title="Guys and Dolls (film)">Guys and Dolls</a></i> (1955). <i>Guys and Dolls</i> would be Brando's first and last musical role. <i>Time</i> found the picture "false to the original in its feeling", remarking that Brando "sings in a faraway tenor that sometimes tends to be flat." Appearing in <a href="/wiki/Edward_R._Murrow" title="Edward R. Murrow">Edward Murrow</a>'s <i>Person to Person</i> interview in early 1955, he admitted to having problems with his singing voice, which he called "pretty terrible." In the 1965 documentary "Meet Marlon Brando" he revealed that the final product heard in the movie was a result of countless singing takes being cut into one and later joked, "I couldn't hit a note with a baseball bat; some notes I missed by extraordinary margins ... They sewed my words together on one song so tightly that when I mouthed it in front of the camera, I nearly asphyxiated myself". Relations between Brando and Sinatra were also frosty, with Stefan Kanfer observing, "The two men were diametrical opposites: Marlon required multiple takes; Frank detested repeating himself." Upon their first meeting Sinatra reportedly scoffed, "Don't give me any of that Actors Studio shit." Brando later famously quipped, "Frank is the kind of guy, when he dies, he's going to heaven and give God a hard time for making him bald." Frank Sinatra famously called Brando "the world's most overrated actor", and referred to him as "mumbles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The film was commercially though not critically successful, costing $5.5 million to make and grossing $13 million.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Brando played Sakini, a Japanese interpreter for the U.S. Army in postwar <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Teahouse_of_the_August_Moon_(film)" title="The Teahouse of the August Moon (film)">The Teahouse of the August Moon</a></i> (1956). <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Kael" title="Pauline Kael">Pauline Kael</a> was not particularly impressed by the movie, but noted "Marlon Brando starved himself to play the pixie interpreter Sakini, and he looks as if he's enjoying the stunt—talking with a mad accent, grinning boyishly, bending forward, and doing tricky movements with his legs. He's harmlessly genial (and he is certainly missed when he's offscreen), though the fey, roguish role doesn't allow him to do what he's great at and it's possible that he's less effective in it than a lesser actor might have been." In <i><a href="/wiki/Sayonara" title="Sayonara">Sayonara</a></i> (1957) he appeared as a United States Air Force officer. <i>Newsweek</i> found the film a "dull tale of the meeting of the twain" but fans disagreed and the movie became a hit. According to Stefan Kanfer's biography of the actor, Marlon's manager Jay Kanter negotiated a profitable contract with ten percent of the gross going to Brando, which put him in the millionaire category. The movie was controversial due to openly discussing <a href="/wiki/Interracial_marriage" title="Interracial marriage">interracial marriage</a> but proved a great success, earning 10 Academy Award nominations, with Brando being nominated for Best Actor. The film went on to win four Academy Awards. <i>Teahouse</i> and <i>Sayonara</i> were the first in a string of films Brando would strive to make over the next decade which contained socially relevant messages, and he formed a partnership with Paramount to establish his own production company called Pennebaker, its declared purpose to develop films that contained "social value that would improve the world." The name was a tribute in honor of his mother, who had died in 1954. By all accounts, Brando was devastated by her death, with biographer Peter Manso telling A&amp;E's <i>Biography</i>, "She was the one who could give him approval like no one else could and, after his mother died, it seems that Marlon stops caring." Brando shocked many who knew him by appointing his hated father to run Pennebaker.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the same A&amp;E special, George Englund claims that Brando did it because "it gave Marlon a chance to take shots at him, to demean and diminish him" while biographer David Thomson speculates that "Brando was psychologically daunted by his father."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1958, Brando appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Young_Lions_(film)" title="The Young Lions (film)">The Young Lions</a></i>, dyeing his hair blonde and assuming a German accent for the role, which he later admitted was not convincing. It based on the novel by <a href="/wiki/Irwin_Shaw" title="Irwin Shaw">Irwin Shaw</a> and Brando's portrayal of the character Christian Diestl was controversial for its time. He later wrote, "The original script closely followed the book, in which Shaw painted all Germans as evil caricatures, especially Christian, whom he portrayed as a symbol of everything that was bad about Nazism; he was mean, nasty, vicious, a cliché of evil ... I thought the story should demonstrate that there are no inherently 'bad' people in the world, but they can easily be misled." Shaw and Brando even appeared together for a televised interview with CBS correspondent <a href="/wiki/David_Schoenbrun" title="David Schoenbrun">David Schoenbrun</a> and, during a bombastic exchange, Shaw charged that, like most actors, Brando was incapable of playing flat-out villainy; Brando responded by stating "Nobody creates a character but an actor. I play the role; now he exists. He is my creation." <i>The Young Lions</i> also features Brando's only appearance in a film with friend and rival <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_Clift" title="Montgomery Clift">Montgomery Clift</a> (although they shared no scenes together). Brando closed out the decade by appearing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fugitive_Kind" title="The Fugitive Kind">The Fugitive Kind</a></i> (1960) opposite <a href="/wiki/Anna_Magnani" title="Anna Magnani">Anna Magnani</a>. The film was based on another play by Tennessee Williams but was hardly the success <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i> had been, with the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> labeling Williams's personae "psychologically sick or just plain ugly" and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> calling it a "cornpone melodrama".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="One-Eyed_Jacks_and_Mutiny_on_the_Bounty"><i>One-Eyed Jacks</i> and <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: One-Eyed Jacks and Mutiny on the Bounty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1961, Brando made his directorial debut in the western <i><a href="/wiki/One-Eyed_Jacks" title="One-Eyed Jacks">One-Eyed Jacks</a></i>. The picture was originally directed by Stanley Kubrick, but he was fired early in the production. Paramount then made Brando the director . Brando portrays the lead character Rio, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Malden" title="Karl Malden">Karl Malden</a> plays his partner "Dad" Longworth. The supporting cast features <a href="/wiki/Katy_Jurado" title="Katy Jurado">Katy Jurado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Johnson_(actor)" title="Ben Johnson (actor)">Ben Johnson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slim_Pickens" title="Slim Pickens">Slim Pickens</a>. Brando's penchant for multiple retakes and character exploration as an actor carried over into his directing, however, and the film soon went over budget; Paramount expected the film to take three months to complete but shooting stretched to six and the cost doubled to more than six million dollars. Brando's inexperience as an editor also delayed postproduction and Paramount eventually took control of the film. Brando later wrote, "Paramount said it didn't like my version of the story; I'd had everyone lie except Karl Malden. The studio cut the movie to pieces and made him a liar, too. By then, I was bored with the whole project and walked away from it."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Visually stunning, <i>One-Eyed Jacks</i> was poorly reviewed by critics. While the film did solid business, it ran so over budget that it lost money.</p>
<p>Brando's revulsion with the film industry reportedly boiled over on the set of his next film, <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty_(1962_film)" title="Mutiny on the Bounty (1962 film)">remake of <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i></a>, which was filmed in <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a>. The actor was accused of deliberately sabotaging nearly every aspect of the production. On June 16, 1962, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Saturday_Evening_Post" title="The Saturday Evening Post">The Saturday Evening Post</a></i> ran an article by Bill Davidson with the headline "Six million dollars down the drain: the mutiny of Marlon Brando". <i>Mutiny</i> director <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Milestone" title="Lewis Milestone">Lewis Milestone</a> claimed that the executives "deserve what they get when they give a ham actor, a petulant child, complete control over an expensive picture." <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i> nearly capsized MGM and, while the project had indeed been hampered with delays other than Brando's behavior, the accusations would dog the actor for years as studios began to fear Brando's difficult reputation. Critics also began taking note of Marlon's fluctuating weight.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box_office_decline:_1963.E2.80.931971">Box office decline: 1963–1971</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Box office decline: 1963–1971">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Distracted by his personal life and becoming disillusioned with his career, Brando began to view acting as a means to an end financially. Critics protested when he started accepting roles in films many perceived as being beneath his talent, or criticized him for failing to live up to the better roles. Previously only signing short term deals with movie studios, in 1961 Brando uncharacteristically signed a five-picture deal with Universal Studios that would haunt him for the rest of the decade. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ugly_American_(film)" title="The Ugly American (film)">The Ugly American</a></i> (1963) was the first of these films. Based on the 1958 novel of the same name that Pennebaker had optioned, the movie, which featured Brando's sister Jocelyn, was rated fairly positively but died at the box office. Brando was nominated for a Golden Globe for his performance. All of Brando's other Universal films during this period, including <i><a href="/wiki/Bedtime_Story_(1964_film)" title="Bedtime Story (1964 film)">Bedtime Story</a></i> (1964), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Appaloosa" title="The Appaloosa">The Appaloosa</a></i> (1966), <i><a href="/wiki/A_Countess_from_Hong_Kong" title="A Countess from Hong Kong">A Countess from Hong Kong</a></i> (1967) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_of_the_Following_Day" title="The Night of the Following Day">The Night of the Following Day</a></i> (1969), were also critical and commercial flops. <i>Countess</i> in particular was a disappointment for Brando, who had looked forward to working with one of his heroes, director <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>. The experience turned out to be an unhappy one; Brando had no chemistry with his leading lady <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Loren" title="Sophia Loren">Sophia Loren</a> and was horrified at Chaplin's didactic style of direction and his authoritarian approach. Brando had also appeared in the spy thriller <i><a href="/wiki/Morituri_(1965_film)" title="Morituri (1965 film)">Morituri</a></i> in 1965; that, too, failed to attract an audience.</p>
<p>Brando acknowledged his professional decline, writing later, "Some of the films I made during the sixties were successful; some weren't. Some, like <i>The Night of the Following Day</i>, I made only for the money; others, like <i><a href="/wiki/Candy_(1968_film)" title="Candy (1968 film)">Candy</a></i>, I did because a friend asked me to and I didn't want to turn him down ... In some ways I think of my middle age as the Fuck You Years." <i>Candy</i> was especially appalling for many; a 1968 sex farce film directed by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Marquand" title="Christian Marquand">Christian Marquand</a> and based on the 1958 novel by <a href="/wiki/Terry_Southern" title="Terry Southern">Terry Southern</a>, the movie satirizes pornographic stories through the adventures of its naive heroine, Candy, played by <a href="/wiki/Ewa_Aulin" title="Ewa Aulin">Ewa Aulin</a>. It is generally regarded as the nadir of Brando's career. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> observed: "Brando's self-indulgence over a dozen years is costing him and his public his talents." In the March 1966 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Kael" title="Pauline Kael">Pauline Kael</a> wrote that in his rebellious days, Marlon "was antisocial because he knew society was crap; he was a hero to youth because he was strong enough not to take the crap" but now Brando and others like him had become "buffoons, shamelessly, pathetically mocking their public reputations." In an earlier review of <i>The Appaloosa</i> in 1966, Kael wrote that the actor was "trapped in another dog of a movie ... Not for the first time, Mr. Brando gives us a heavy-lidded, adenoidally openmouthed caricature of the inarticulate, stalwart loner." Although he feigned indifference, Brando was hurt by the critical mauling, admitting in the 2015 film <i><a href="/wiki/Listen_to_Me_Marlon" title="Listen to Me Marlon">Listen to Me Marlon</a></i>, "They can hit you every day and you have no way of fighting back. I was very convincing in my pose of indifference, but I was very sensitive and it hurt a lot."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (February 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>While Brando had lost much of his critical and commercial appeal in the 1960s, he still gave some memorable performances. Brando portrayed a repressed gay army officer in <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_in_a_Golden_Eye_(film)" title="Reflections in a Golden Eye (film)">Reflections in a Golden Eye</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/John_Huston" title="John Huston">John Huston</a> and costarring <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Taylor" title="Elizabeth Taylor">Elizabeth Taylor</a>. The role turned out as one of his greatest in years, with Stanley Crouch marveling, "Brando's main achievement was to portray the taciturn but stoic gloom of those pulverized by circumstances."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> The film overall received mixed reviews. Another notable film was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chase_(1966_film)" title="The Chase (1966 film)">The Chase</a></i> (1966), which paired the actor with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Penn" title="Arthur Penn">Arthur Penn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Duvall" title="Robert Duvall">Robert Duvall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Fonda" title="Jane Fonda">Jane Fonda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Redford" title="Robert Redford">Robert Redford</a>. The film deals with themes of racism, sexual revolution, small-town corruption, and vigilantism but is probably best remembered for a scene in which the sheriff played by Brando is brutally beaten by three of the vigilantes. The film was received mostly positively.</p>
<p>Brando cited <i><a href="/wiki/Burn!" title="Burn!">Burn!</a></i> (1969) as his personal favorite of the films he had made, writing in his autobiography, "I think I did some of the best acting I've ever done in that picture, but few people came to see it." Brando dedicated a full chapter to the film in his memoir, stating that the director, <a href="/wiki/Gillo_Pontecorvo" title="Gillo Pontecorvo">Gillo Pontecorvo</a>, was the best director he had ever worked with next to Kazan and <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Bertolucci" title="Bernardo Bertolucci">Bernardo Bertolucci</a>. Brando also detailed his clashes with Pontecorvo on the set and how "we nearly killed each other." Loosely based on events in the history of Guadeloupe, the film got a hostile reception from critics despite a committed performance from Brando. In 1971, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Winner" title="Michael Winner">Michael Winner</a> directed him in the British horror film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nightcomers" title="The Nightcomers">The Nightcomers</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Beacham" title="Stephanie Beacham">Stephanie Beacham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thora_Hird" title="Thora Hird">Thora Hird</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Andrews" title="Harry Andrews">Harry Andrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anna_Palk" title="Anna Palk">Anna Palk</a>. It is a prequel to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Turn_of_the_Screw" title="The Turn of the Screw">The Turn of the Screw</a></i>, which later became the 1961 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Innocents_(1961_film)" title="The Innocents (1961 film)">The Innocents</a></i>. Brando's performance earned him a nomination for a Best Actor BAFTA but the film bombed at the box office.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Godfather_and_Last_Tango_in_Paris"><i>The Godfather</i> and <i>Last Tango in Paris</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Godfather and Last Tango in Paris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1970s, Brando was considered "unbankable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Critics were becoming increasingly dismissive of his work and he had not appeared in a box office hit since <i>The Young Lions</i> in 1958, the last year he had ranked as one of the Top Ten Box Office Stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> and the year of his last Academy Award nomination, for <i>Sayonara.</i> Brando's performance as <a href="/wiki/Vito_Corleone" title="Vito Corleone">Vito Corleone</a>, the "Don," in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">The Godfather</a></i> (1972), <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">Francis Ford Coppola</a>'s adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Mario_Puzo" title="Mario Puzo">Mario Puzo</a>'s 1969 best-selling <a href="/wiki/The_Godfather_(novel)" title="The Godfather (novel)">novel of the same name</a>, was a career turning point, putting him back in the Top Ten and winning him his second Best Actor Oscar.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount</a> production chief <a href="/wiki/Robert_Evans" title="Robert Evans">Robert Evans</a>, who had given Puzo an advance to write <i>The Godfather</i> so that Paramount would own the movie rights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> hired Coppola after many major directors had turned the movie down because he wanted an Italian-American director who could provide the movie with cultural authenticity. Coppola also came cheap. Evans was conscious of the fact that Paramount's last Mafia movie, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brotherhood_(1968_film)" title="The Brotherhood (1968 film)">The Brotherhood</a></i> (1968) had been a box office bomb, and he believed it was partly due to the fact that the director, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Ritt" title="Martin Ritt">Martin Ritt</a>, and the star, <a href="/wiki/Kirk_Douglas" title="Kirk Douglas">Kirk Douglas</a>, were Jews and the film lacked an authentic Italian flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> The studio originally intended the movie to be a low-budget production set in contemporary times without any major actors, but the phenomenal success of the novel gave Evans the clout to turn <i>The Godfather</i> into a prestige picture.</p>
<p>Coppola had developed a list of actors for all the roles, and his list of potential Dons included the Oscar-winning Italian-American <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Borgnine" title="Ernest Borgnine">Ernest Borgnine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> the Italian-American <a href="/wiki/Frank_de_Kova" title="Frank de Kova">Frank de Kova</a> (best known for playing Chief Wild Eagle on the TV sit-com <i><a href="/wiki/F_Troop" title="F Troop">F-Troop</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/John_Marley" title="John Marley">John Marley</a> (a Best Supporting Oscar-nominee for Paramount's 1970 hit movie <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Story_(1970_film)" title="Love Story (1970 film)">Love Story</a></i> who was cast as the movie producer Woltz in the picture), the Italian-American <a href="/wiki/Richard_Conte" title="Richard Conte">Richard Conte</a> (who was cast as Don Corleone's deadly rival Don Barzini), and Italian movie producer <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Ponti" title="Carlo Ponti">Carlo Ponti</a>. Coppola admitted in a 1975 interview, "We finally figured we had to lure the <i>best</i> actor in the world. It was that simple. That boiled down to <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> or Marlon Brando, who <i>are</i> the greatest actors in the world." The holographic copy of Coppola's cast list shows Brando's name underlined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evans told Coppola that he had been thinking of Brando for the part two years earlier, and Puzo had imagined Brando in the part when he wrote the novel and had actually written to him about the part,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> so Coppola and Evans narrowed it down to Brando.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> (Ironically, Olivier would compete with Brando for the Best Actor Oscar for his part in <i><a href="/wiki/Sleuth_(1972_film)" title="Sleuth (1972 film)">Sleuth</a>.</i> He bested Brando at the <a href="/wiki/1972_New_York_Film_Critics_Circle_Awards" title="1972 New York Film Critics Circle Awards">1972 New York Film Critics Circle Awards</a>.) <a href="/wiki/Albert_S._Ruddy" title="Albert S. Ruddy">Albert S. Ruddy</a>, who Paramount assigned to produce the film, agreed with the choice of Brando. However, Paramount studio heads were opposed to casting Brando due to his reputation for difficulty and his long string of box office flops. Brando also had <i>One-Eyed Jacks</i> working against him, a troubled production that lost money for Paramount when it was released in 1961. Paramount Pictures President <a href="/wiki/Stanley_R._Jaffe" title="Stanley R. Jaffe">Stanley Jaffe</a> told an exasperated Coppola, "As long as I'm president of this studio, Marlon Brando will not be in this picture, and I will no longer allow you to discuss it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jaffe eventually set three conditions for the casting of Brando: That he would have to take a fee far below what he typically received; he'd have to agree to accept financial responsibility for any production delays his behavior cost; and he had to submit to a screen test. In order to skirt the humiliation of having Brando submit to a screen test, Coppola convinced Brando to a videotaped "make-up" test, in which Brando did his own makeup (he used cotton balls to simulate the puffed-cheek look). Coppola had feared Brando might be too young to play the Don, but was electrified by the actor's characterization as the head of a crime family. Even so, he had to fight the studio in order to cast the temperamental actor. Brando had doubts himself, stating in his autobiography, "I had never played an Italian before, and I didn't think I could do it successfully." Eventually, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bluhdorn" title="Charles Bluhdorn">Charles Bluhdorn</a>, the president of Paramount parent <a href="/wiki/Gulf_and_Western_Industries" title="Gulf and Western Industries">Gulf+Western</a>, was won over to letting Brando have the role; when he saw the screen test, he asked in amazement, "What are we watching? Who is this old guinea?" Brando was signed for a low fee of $50,000, but in his contract, he was given a percentage of the gross on a sliding scale: 1% of the gross for each $10 million over a $10 million threshold, up to 5% if the picture exceeded $60 million. According to Evans, Brando sold back his points in the picture for $100,000 as he was in dire need of funds. "That $100,000 cost him $11 million," Evans claimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 1994 interview that can be found on the Academy of Achievement website, Coppola insisted, "<i>The Godfather</i> was a very unappreciated movie when we were making it. They were very unhappy with it. They didn't like the cast. They didn't like the way I was shooting it. I was always on the verge of getting fired." When word of this reached Brando, he threatened to walk off the picture, writing in his memoir, "I strongly believe that directors are entitled to independence and freedom to realize their vision, though Francis left the characterizations in our hands and we had to figure out what to do." In a 2010 television interview with <a href="/wiki/Larry_King" title="Larry King">Larry King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Pacino" title="Al Pacino">Al Pacino</a> also talked about how Brando's support helped him keep the role of Michael Corleone in the movie—despite the fact director Francis Ford Coppola wanted to sack him. Brando was on his best behavior during filming, buoyed by an impressive cast that included Al Pacino, Robert Duvall, <a href="/wiki/James_Caan" title="James Caan">James Caan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diane_Keaton" title="Diane Keaton">Diane Keaton</a>. In the <i>Vanity Fair</i> article "The Godfather Wars" Mark Seals writes, "With the actors, as in the movie, Brando served as the head of the family. He broke the ice by toasting the group with a glass of wine. 'When we were young, Brando was like the godfather of actors,' says Robert Duvall. 'I used to meet with <a href="/wiki/Dustin_Hoffman" title="Dustin Hoffman">Dustin Hoffman</a> in Cromwell's Drugstore, and if we mentioned his name once, we mentioned it 25 times in a day.' Caan adds, 'The first day we met Brando everybody was in awe.'"</p>
<p>Brando's performance was glowingly reviewed by critics. "I thought it would be interesting to play a gangster, maybe for the first time in the movies, who wasn't like those bad guys <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Robinson" title="Edward G. Robinson">Edward G. Robinson</a> played, but who is kind of a hero, a man to be respected," Brando recalled in his autobiography. "Also, because he had so much power and unquestioned authority, I thought it would be an interesting contrast to play him as a gentle man, unlike <a href="/wiki/Al_Capone" title="Al Capone">Al Capone</a>, who beat up people with baseball bats." Co-star Robert Duvall later marveled to A&amp;E's <i>Biography</i>, "He minimized the sense of beginning. In other words he, like, deemphasized the word <i>action</i>. He would go in front of that camera just like he was before. <i>Cut!</i> It was all the same. There was really no beginning. I learned a lot from watching that." Brando won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Academy Award for Best Actor</a> for his performance, but he declined it, becoming the second actor to refuse a Best Actor award (after <a href="/wiki/George_C._Scott" title="George C. Scott">George C. Scott</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/Patton_(film)" title="Patton (film)">Patton</a></i>). He boycotted the award ceremony, instead sending American Indian rights activist <a href="/wiki/Sacheen_Littlefeather" title="Sacheen Littlefeather">Sacheen Littlefeather</a>, who appeared in full Apache attire, to state Brando's reasons, which were based on his objection to the depiction of American Indians by Hollywood and television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The actor followed <i>The Godfather</i> with Bernardo Bertolucci's 1972 film <i>Last Tango in Paris</i> opposite <a href="/wiki/Maria_Schneider_(actress)" title="Maria Schneider (actress)">Maria Schneider</a>, but Brando's highly noted performance threatened to be overshadowed by an uproar over the sexual content of the film. Brando portrays a recent American widower named Paul, who begins an anonymous sexual relationship with a young, betrothed Parisian woman named Jeanne. As with previous films, Brando refused to memorize his lines for many scenes; instead, he wrote his lines on cue cards and posted them around the set for easy reference, leaving Bertolucci with the problem of keeping them out of the picture frame. The film features several intense, graphic scenes involving Brando, including Paul anally raping Jeanne using butter as a lubricant and Paul's angry, emotionally charged final confrontation with the corpse of his dead wife. The controversial movie was a hit, however, and Brando made the list of Top Ten Box Office Stars for the last time. The voting membership of the Academy of Motion Picture Arts &amp; Sciences again nominated Brando for Best Actor, his seventh nomination. Although Brando won the 1973 New York Film Critics Circle Awards, the actor did not appear at the ceremony or send a representative to pick up the award if he won.</p>
<p>Critic <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Kael" title="Pauline Kael">Pauline Kael</a>, in her famous <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">New Yorker</a></i> review, wrote "The movie breakthrough has finally come. Bertolucci and Brando have altered the face of an art form." Brando confessed in his autobiography, "To this day I can't say what <i>Last Tango in Paris</i> was about," and added the film "required me to do a lot of emotional arm wrestling with myself, and when it was finished, I decided that I wasn't ever again going to destroy myself emotionally to make a movie. I felt I had violated my innermost self and I didn't want to suffer like that anymore ...
