You can't fake it."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (February 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1973, Brando was devastated by the death of his childhood and best friend <a href="/wiki/Wally_Cox" title="Wally Cox">Wally Cox</a>. Brando slept in Cox's pajamas and wrenched his ashes from his widow. She was going to sue for their return, but finally said "I think Marlon needs the ashes more than I do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_1970s">Late 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Late 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1976, Brando appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Missouri_Breaks" title="The Missouri Breaks">The Missouri Breaks</a></i> with his friend <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">Jack Nicholson</a>. The movie also reunited the actor with director Arthur Penn. Following <i>The Godfather</i> and <i>Tango</i>, Brando's performance was disappointing for some reviewers, who accused him of giving an erratic and inconsistent performance. As biographer Stefan Kanfer describes, Penn had difficulty controlling Brando, who seemed intent on going over the top with his border-ruffian-turned-contract-killer Robert E. Lee Clayton: "Marlon made him a cross-dressing psychopath. Absent for the first hour of the movie, Clayton enters on horseback, dangling upside down, caparisoned in white buckskin, Littlefeather-style. He speaks in an Irish accent for no apparent reason. Over the next hour, also for no apparent reason, Clayton assumes the intonation of a British upper-class twit and an elderly frontier woman, complete with a granny dress and matching bonnet. Penn, who believed in letting actors do their thing, indulged Marlon all the way." Critics were unkind, with <i>The Observer</i> calling Brando's performance "one of the most extravagant displays of <i>grandedamerie</i> since <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Bernhardt" title="Sarah Bernhardt">Sarah Bernhardt</a>", while <i>The Sun</i> complained, "Marlon Brando at fifty-two has the sloppy belly of a sixty-two-year-old, the white hair of a seventy-two-year-old, and the lack of discipline of a precocious twelve-year-old."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (January 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup> However, Kanfer noted: "Even though his late work was met with disapproval, a re-examination shows that often, in the middle of the most pedestrian scene, there would be a sudden, luminous occurrence, a flash of the old Marlon that showed how capable he remained."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (January 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jor-EL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="251" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Jor-EL.jpg/170px-Jor-EL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e9/Jor-EL.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jor-EL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brando as <a href="/wiki/Jor-El" title="Jor-El">Jor-El</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Superman_(1978_film)" title="Superman (1978 film)">Superman</a></i> (1978).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1977, Brando made a rare television appearance in the miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/Roots:_The_Next_Generations" title="Roots: The Next Generations">Roots: The Next Generations</a></i>, portraying <a href="/wiki/George_Lincoln_Rockwell" title="George Lincoln Rockwell">George Lincoln Rockwell</a>; he won a <a href="/wiki/Primetime_Emmy_Award_for_Outstanding_Supporting_Actor_in_a_Limited_Series_or_Movie" title="Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Supporting Actor in a Limited Series or Movie">Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Supporting Actor in a Miniseries or a Movie</a> for his performance. In 1978, he narrated the English version of <i><a href="/wiki/Raoni" title="Raoni">Raoni</a></i>, a French-Belgian documentary film directed by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Dutilleux" title="Jean-Pierre Dutilleux">Jean-Pierre Dutilleux</a> and Luiz Carlos Saldanha that focused on the life of <a href="/wiki/Raoni_Metuktire" title="Raoni Metuktire">Raoni Metuktire</a> and issues surrounding the survival of the indigenous Indian tribes of north central Brazil. Brando portrayed Superman's father <a href="/wiki/Jor-El" title="Jor-El">Jor-El</a> in the 1978 film <i>Superman</i>. He agreed to the role only on assurance that he would be paid a large sum for what amounted to a small part, that he would not have to read the script beforehand, and that his lines would be displayed somewhere off-camera. It was revealed in a documentary contained in the 2001 DVD release of <i>Superman</i> that he was paid $3.7 million for two weeks of work. Brando also filmed scenes for the movie's sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Superman_II" title="Superman II">Superman II</a></i>, but after producers refused to pay him the same percentage he received for the first movie, he denied them permission to use the footage. "I asked for my usual percentage," he recollected in his memoir, "but they refused, and so did I." However, after Brando's death, the footage was reincorporated into the 2006 re-cut of the film, <i><a href="/wiki/Superman_II:_The_Richard_Donner_Cut" title="Superman II: The Richard Donner Cut">Superman II: The Richard Donner Cut</a></i> and in the 2006 "loose sequel" <i><a href="/wiki/Superman_Returns" title="Superman Returns">Superman Returns</a></i>, in which both used and unused archive footage of him as Jor-El from the first two <i>Superman</i> films was remastered for a scene in the Fortress of Solitude, and Brando's voice-overs were used throughout the film. As the decade wore on, Brando was criticized more and more for only accepting what amounted to cameos in exchange for huge paydays.</p>
<p>Brando starred as <a href="/wiki/Colonel_Kurtz" title="Colonel Kurtz">Colonel Walter E. Kurtz</a> in Francis Ford Coppola's Vietnam epic <i><a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_Now" title="Apocalypse Now">Apocalypse Now</a></i> (1979). He plays a highly decorated U.S. Army Special Forces officer who goes renegade, running his own operation based in Cambodia and is feared by the U.S. military as much as the Vietnamese. Brando was paid $1 million a week for 3 weeks work. The film drew attention for its lengthy and troubled production, as <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Coppola" title="Eleanor Coppola">Eleanor Coppola</a>'s documentary <i>Hearts of Darkness: A Filmmaker's Apocalypse</i> documents: Brando showed up on the set overweight, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Sheen" title="Martin Sheen">Martin Sheen</a> suffered a heart attack, and severe weather destroyed several expensive sets. The film's release was also postponed several times while Coppola edited millions of feet of footage. In the documentary, Coppola talks about how astonished he was when an overweight Brando turned up for his scenes and, feeling desperate, decided to portray Kurtz, who appears emaciated in the original story, as a man who had indulged every aspect of himself. Coppola: "He was already heavy when I hired him and he promised me that he was going to get in shape and I imagined that I would, if he were heavy, I could use that. But he was <i>so</i> fat, he was very, very shy about it ... He was very, very adamant about how he didn't want to portray himself that way." Brando admitted to Coppola that he had not read the book, <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Darkness" title="Heart of Darkness">Heart of Darkness</a></i>, as the director had asked him to, and the pair spent days exploring the story and the character of Kurtz, much to the actor's financial benefit, according to producer <a href="/wiki/Fred_Roos" title="Fred Roos">Fred Roos</a>: "The clock was ticking on this deal he had and we had to finish him within three weeks or we'd go into this very expensive overage ... And Francis and Marlon would be talking about the character and whole days would go by. And this is at Marlon's urging—and yet he's getting paid for it."</p>
<p>Upon release, <i>Apocalypse Now</i> earned critical acclaim, as did Brando's performance. Marlon's whispering of Kurtz's final words <i>"The horror! The horror!"</i>, has become particularly famous. Roger Ebert, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Sun-Times" title="Chicago Sun-Times">Chicago Sun-Times</a></i>, defended the movie's controversial <i>denouement</i>, opining that the ending, "with Brando's fuzzy, brooding monologues and final violence, feels more satisfactory than any conventional ending possibly could."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (January 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_work">Later work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Later work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After appearing as oil tycoon Adam Steiffel in 1980's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Formula_(1980_film)" title="The Formula (1980 film)">The Formula</a></i>, which was poorly received critically, Brando announced his retirement from acting. However he returned in 1989 in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dry_White_Season" title="A Dry White Season">A Dry White Season</a></i>, based on <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Brink" title="André Brink">André Brink</a>'s 1979 <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">anti-apartheid</a> novel. Brando agreed to do the film for free, but fell out with director <a href="/wiki/Euzhan_Palcy" title="Euzhan Palcy">Euzhan Palcy</a> over how the film was edited; he even made a rare television appearance in an interview with <a href="/wiki/Connie_Chung" title="Connie Chung">Connie Chung</a> to voice his disapproval. In his memoir, he maintained that Palcy "had cut the picture so poorly, I thought, that the inherent drama of this conflict was vague at best." Brando received praise for his performance, earning an Academy Award nomination for Best Supporting Actor and winning the Best Actor Award at the Tokyo Film Festival. Brando also scored enthusiastic reviews for his caricature of his Vito Corleone role as Carmine Sabatini in 1990's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Freshman_(1990_film)" title="The Freshman (1990 film)">The Freshman</a>.</i> In his original review, Roger Ebert wrote, "There have been a lot of movies where stars have repeated the triumphs of their parts—but has any star ever done it more triumphantly than Marlon Brando does in <i>The Freshman</i>?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> Variety also praised Brando's performance as Sabatini and noted, "Marlon Brando's sublime comedy performance elevates <i>The Freshman</i> from screwball comedy to a quirky niche in film history."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE" title="Wikipedia:MOSQUOTE"><span title="The text near this tag needs citation. (January 2016)">this quote needs a citation</span></a></i>]</sup> Brando also starred alongside his friend <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Depp" title="Johnny Depp">Johnny Depp</a> in the box office hit <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Juan_DeMarco" title="Don Juan DeMarco">Don Juan DeMarco</a></i> (1995) and in Depp's controversial <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brave" title="The Brave">The Brave</a></i> (1997), which was never released in the United States. Later performances, such as his appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus:_The_Discovery" title="Christopher Columbus: The Discovery">Christopher Columbus: The Discovery</a></i> (1992) (for which he won a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Raspberry_Awards" title="Golden Raspberry Awards">Raspberry</a> as "Worst Supporting Actor"), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_of_Dr._Moreau_(1996_film)" title="The Island of Dr. Moreau (1996 film)">The Island of Dr. Moreau</a></i> (in which he was nominated for another "Worst Actor" Raspberry) (1996), and his barely recognizable appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Free_Money_(film)" title="Free Money (film)">Free Money</a></i> (1998), resulted in some of the worst reviews of his career. However, his last completed film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Score_(2001_film)" title="The Score (2001 film)">The Score</a></i> (2001), was received generally positively. In the film, in which he portrays a fence, he starred with <a href="/wiki/Robert_De_Niro" title="Robert De Niro">Robert De Niro</a>, who had portrayed Vito Corleone in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather_Part_II" title="The Godfather Part II">The Godfather Part II</a></i>. Brando conceived the idea of a novel called <i>Fan-Tan</i> with director <a href="/wiki/Donald_Cammell" title="Donald Cammell">Donald Cammell</a> in 1979, which was not released until 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Final years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brando's notoriety, his troubled family life, and his <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> attracted more attention than his late acting career. He gained a great deal of weight in the 1960s and by the early to mid-1990s he weighed over 300 lbs (136 kg) and suffered from <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">Type 2 diabetes</a>. He had a history of weight fluctuation throughout his career that, by and large, he attributed to his years of stress-related overeating followed by compensatory dieting. He also earned a reputation for being difficult on the set, often unwilling or unable to memorize his lines and less interested in taking direction than in confronting the film director with odd demands.</p>
<p>He also dabbled with some innovation in his last years. He had several patents issued in his name from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">U.S. Patent and Trademark Office</a>, all of which involve a method of tensioning drum heads, in June 2002 – November 2004. (For example, see <span><a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US6812392" rel="nofollow">U.S. Patent 6,812,392</a></span> and its equivalents).</p>
<p>In 2004, Brando recorded voice tracks for the character Mrs. Sour in the unreleased animated film <i><a href="/wiki/Big_Bug_Man" title="Big Bug Man">Big Bug Man</a></i>. This was his last role and his only role as a female character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The actor was a longtime close friend of entertainer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a> and paid regular visits to his <a href="/wiki/Neverland_Ranch" title="Neverland Ranch">Neverland Ranch</a>, resting there for weeks at a time. Brando also participated in the singer's two-day solo career 30th-anniversary celebration concerts in 2001, and starred in his 13-minute-long music video, "<a href="/wiki/You_Rock_My_World" title="You Rock My World">You Rock My World</a>," in the same year. On Jackson's 30th anniversary concert, Brando gave a rambling speech to the audience on humanitarian work which received a poor reaction and was unaired.</p>
<p>The actor's son, Miko, was Jackson's bodyguard and assistant for several years, and was a friend of the singer. He stated, "The last time my father left his house to go anywhere, to spend any kind of time ... was with Michael Jackson. He loved it ... He had a 24-hour chef, 24-hour security, 24-hour help, 24-hour kitchen, 24-hour maid service."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> "Michael was instrumental helping my father through the last few years of his life. For that I will always be indebted to him. Dad had a hard time breathing in his final days, and he was on oxygen much of the time. He loved the outdoors, so Michael would invite him over to Neverland. Dad could name all the trees there, and the flowers, but being on oxygen it was hard for him to get around and see them all, it's such a big place. So Michael got Dad a golf cart with a portable oxygen tank so he could go around and enjoy Neverland. They'd just drive around—Michael Jackson, Marlon Brando, with an oxygen tank in a golf cart."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2001, Brando was hospitalized with <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, Brando signed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunisian_people" title="Tunisian people">Tunisian</a> film director Ridha Behi and began pre-production on a project to be titled <i>Brando and Brando</i>. Up to a week before his death, he was working on the script in anticipation of a July/August 2004 start date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irelandonline_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-irelandonline-57">[55]</a></sup> Production was suspended in July 2004 following Brando's death, at which time Behi stated that he would continue the film as an homage to Brando,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foxnews1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-foxnews1-58">[56]</a></sup> with a new title of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen_Brando" title="Citizen Brando">Citizen Brando</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-variety1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-variety1-60">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 1, 2004, Brando died of <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a> from <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">pulmonary fibrosis</a> with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">congestive heart failure</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_UCLA_Medical_Center" title="Ronald Reagan UCLA Medical Center">UCLA Medical Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> He left behind 14 children (two of his children, <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_Brando" title="Cheyenne Brando">Cheyenne</a> and Dylan Brando, had predeceased him), as well as over 30 grandchildren. He was also survived by his sister <a href="/wiki/Jocelyn_Brando" title="Jocelyn Brando">Jocelyn</a>. The cause of death was initially withheld, with his lawyer citing privacy concerns. He also suffered from failing eyesight caused by <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liver_cancer" title="Liver cancer">liver cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> Shortly before his death and despite needing an oxygen mask to breathe, he recorded his voice to appear in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather_(2006_video_game)" title="The Godfather (2006 video game)">The Godfather: The Game</a></i>, once again as Don Vito Corleone. However, Brando only recorded one line due to his health and an impersonator was hired to finish his lines. Some lines from his character were directly lifted from <a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">the film</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Karl_Malden" title="Karl Malden">Karl Malden</a>—a fellow actor in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1951_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1951 film)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">On the Waterfront</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/One-Eyed_Jacks" title="One-Eyed Jacks">One-Eyed Jacks</a></i> (the only film directed by Brando)—talks in a documentary accompanying the DVD of <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i> about a phone call he received from Brando shortly before Brando's death. A distressed Brando told Malden he kept falling over. Malden wanted to come over, but Brando put him off, telling him there was no point. Three weeks later, Brando was dead. Shortly before his death, he had apparently refused permission for tubes carrying oxygen to be inserted into his lungs, which, he was told, was the only way to prolong his life.</p>
<p>Brando was <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremated</a>, and his ashes were put in with those of his childhood friend, comedian and actor <a href="/wiki/Wally_Cox" title="Wally Cox">Wally Cox</a> and another longtime friend, Sam Gilman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> They were then scattered partly in <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> and partly in <a href="/wiki/Death_Valley" title="Death Valley">Death Valley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> In 2007, a 165-minute biopic of Brando for <a href="/wiki/Turner_Classic_Movies" title="Turner Classic Movies">Turner Classic Movies</a>, <i>Brando: The Documentary</i>, produced by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Medavoy" title="Mike Medavoy">Mike Medavoy</a> (the executor of Brando's will), was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="NavFrame" style="margin: 0 0 1.5em 1em; background: #f9f9f9; float: right; width: 20em;">

<p>Brando was known for his tumultuous personal life and his large number of wives, girlfriends and children. He was the father to sixteen known children, three of whom were adopted. Some sources claim he fathered as many as 17 children or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marlon_Brando_17_children._2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marlon_Brando_17_children.-2">[2]</a></sup> In 1976, he told a French journalist, "Homosexuality is so much in fashion, it no longer makes news. Like a large number of men, I, too, have had homosexual experiences, and I am not ashamed. I have never paid much attention to what people think about me. But if there is someone who is convinced that <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">Jack Nicholson</a> and I are lovers, may they continue to do so. I find it amusing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-queers_in_history_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-queers_in_history-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>Songs My Mother Taught Me</i>, Brando wrote he met <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Monroe" title="Marilyn Monroe">Marilyn Monroe</a> at a party where she played piano, unnoticed by anybody else there, that they had an affair and maintained an intermittent relationship for many years, and that he received a telephone call from her several days before she died. He also claimed numerous other romances, although he did not discuss his marriages, his wives, or his children in his autobiography. As a young man he was a family friend of <a href="/wiki/Stella_Adler" title="Stella Adler">Stella Adler</a>'s, whose daughter Ellen was a roommate of novelist <a href="/wiki/Paula_Fox" title="Paula Fox">Paula Fox</a>. For a while, Brando and Fox lived under one roof and became close. Brando may have been the father of Fox's first child, daughter <a href="/wiki/Linda_Carroll" title="Linda Carroll">Linda Carroll</a>, who was born in 1944. Linda was given up for adoption and is the mother of <a href="/wiki/Courtney_Love" title="Courtney Love">Courtney Love</a>. Fox never named the child's father or addressed the affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando married actress <a href="/wiki/Anna_Kashfi" title="Anna Kashfi">Anna Kashfi</a> in 1957. Kashfi was born in Calcutta and moved to Wales from India in 1947. She is said to have been the daughter of a Welsh steel worker of Irish descent, William O'Callaghan, who had been superintendent on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Railways" title="Indian Railways">Indian State railways</a>. However, in her book, <i>Brando for Breakfast</i>, she claimed that she really is half Indian and that the press incorrectly thought that her stepfather, O'Callaghan, was her biological father. She said that her biological father was Indian and that she was the result of an "unregistered alliance" between her parents. Brando and Kashfi had a son, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Brando" title="Christian Brando">Christian Brando</a>, on May 11, 1958; they divorced in 1959.</p>
<p>In 1960, Brando married <a href="/wiki/Movita_Castaneda" title="Movita Castaneda">Movita Castaneda</a>, a Mexican-American actress seven years his senior; they were divorced in 1962. Castaneda had appeared in the first <i><a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty_(1935_film)" title="Mutiny on the Bounty (1935 film)">Mutiny on the Bounty</a></i> film in 1935, some 27 years before the <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_on_the_Bounty_(1962_film)" title="Mutiny on the Bounty (1962 film)">1962 remake</a> with Brando as <a href="/wiki/Fletcher_Christian" title="Fletcher Christian">Fletcher Christian</a>. They had two children together: Miko Castaneda Brando (born 1961) and Rebecca Brando (born 1966).</p>
<p>Tahitian actress <a href="/wiki/Tarita_Teriipaia" title="Tarita Teriipaia">Tarita Teriipaia</a>, who played Brando's love interest in <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i>, became his third wife on August 10, 1962. She was 20 years old, 18 years younger than Brando, who was reportedly delighted by her naïveté.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> Because Teriipaia was a native French speaker, Brando became fluent in the language and gave numerous interviews in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailymotion_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-dailymotion-74">[72]</a></sup> Teriipaia became the mother of two of his children: Simon Teihotu Brando (born 1963) and <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_Brando" title="Cheyenne Brando">Tarita Cheyenne Brando</a> (born 1970). Brando also adopted Teriipaia's daughter, Maimiti Brando (born 1977) and niece, Raiatua Brando (born 1982). Brando and Teriipaia divorced in July 1972.</p>
<p>Brando had a long-term relationship with his housekeeper Maria Cristina Ruiz, by whom he had three children: Ninna Priscilla Brando (born May 13, 1989), Myles Jonathan Brando (born January 16, 1992), and Timothy Gahan Brando (born January 6, 1994). He had five more children by unidentified women: <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Blackehart" title="Stephen Blackehart">Stephen Blackehart</a> (born 1967),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Michael Gregor Gilman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> (born 1967), who was adopted by Brando's longtime friend Sam Gilman, and Dylan Brando (1968–1988), Warren Angelo Brando<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> (born 1985), Angelique Brando. Brando also adopted Petra Brando-Corval (born 1972), the daughter of his assistant Caroline Barrett and novelist <a href="/wiki/James_Clavell" title="James Clavell">James Clavell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando's close friendship with Wally Cox was the subject of rumors. Brando told a journalist: "If Wally had been a woman, I would have married him and we would have lived happily ever after".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> Two of Cox's wives, however, dismissed the suggestion that the love was more than platonic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando's grandson <a href="/wiki/Tuki_Brando" title="Tuki Brando">Tuki Brando</a> (born 1990), son of <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_Brando" title="Cheyenne Brando">Cheyenne Brando</a>, is a fashion model. His numerous grandchildren also include Michael Brando (born 1988), son of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Brando" title="Christian Brando">Christian Brando</a>, Prudence Brando and Shane Brando, children of Miko C. Brando, the children of Rebecca Brando,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> the three children of Teihotu Brando and the children of Michael Gregor Gilman,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_Dag_Drollet">Death of Dag Drollet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Death of Dag Drollet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 1990, Dag Drollet, the Tahitian lover of Brando's daughter <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_Brando" title="Cheyenne Brando">Cheyenne</a>, died of a gunshot wound after a confrontation with Cheyenne's half-brother <a href="/wiki/Christian_Brando" title="Christian Brando">Christian</a> at the family's hilltop home above <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">Beverly Hills</a>. Christian, then 31 years old, claimed he was drunk and the shooting was accidental. After heavily publicized pre-trial proceedings, Christian pleaded guilty to <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_manslaughter" title="Voluntary manslaughter">voluntary manslaughter</a> and use of a gun. He was sentenced to 10 years in prison. Before the sentence, Brando delivered an hour of testimony, in which he said he and his former wife had failed Christian. He commented softly to members of the Drollet family: "I'm sorry ... If I could trade places with Dag, I would. I'm prepared for the consequences." Afterward, Drollet's father, Jacques, said he thought Brando was acting and his son was "getting away with murder." The tragedy was compounded in 1995, when Cheyenne, suffering from lingering effects of a serious car accident and said to still be depressed over Drollet's death, committed suicide by hanging herself in Tahiti. Christian Brando died of pneumonia at age 49 on January 26, 2008.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle">Lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brando earned a reputation as a '<a href="/wiki/Bad_boy_(archetype)" title="Bad boy (archetype)">bad boy</a>' for his public outbursts and antics. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_(magazine)" title="Los Angeles (magazine)">Los Angeles</a></i> magazine, "Brando was rock and roll before anybody knew what rock and roll was".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> His behavior during the filming of <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i> (1962) seemed to bolster his reputation as a difficult star. He was blamed for a change in director and a runaway budget, though he disclaimed responsibility for either. On June 12, 1973, Brando broke <a href="/wiki/Paparazzi" title="Paparazzi">paparazzo</a> <a href="/wiki/Ron_Galella" title="Ron Galella">Ron Galella</a>'s jaw. Galella had followed Brando, who was accompanied by talk show host <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cavett" title="Dick Cavett">Dick Cavett</a>, after a taping of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dick_Cavett_Show" title="The Dick Cavett Show">The Dick Cavett Show</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. He reportedly paid a $40,000 out-of-court settlement and suffered an infected hand as a result. Galella wore a football helmet the next time he photographed Brando at a gala benefiting the American Indians Development Association.</p>
<p>The filming of <i>Mutiny on the Bounty</i> affected Brando's life in a profound way, as he fell in love with Tahiti and its people. He bought a 12-island atoll, <a href="/wiki/Tetiaroa" title="Tetiaroa">Tetiaroa</a>, and in 1970 hired an award-winning young Los Angeles architect, Bernard Judge, to build his home and natural village there without despoiling the environment. An environmental laboratory protecting sea birds and turtles was established and student groups welcomed there for many years. Tragically, the 1983 hurricane destroyed many of the structures including his resort. A hotel using Brando's name is currently under reconstruction under new ownership due to open in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> His son Simon is the only inhabitant of <a href="/wiki/Tetiaroa" title="Tetiaroa">Tetiaroa</a>. Brando was an active <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio" title="Amateur radio">ham radio</a> operator, with the <a href="/wiki/Call_sign" title="Call sign">call signs</a> KE6PZH and FO5GJ (the latter from his island). He was listed in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) records as Martin Brandeaux to preserve his privacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the A&amp;E <i>Biography</i> episode on Brando, biographer Peter Manso comments, "On the one hand, being a celebrity allowed Marlon to take his revenge on the world that had so deeply hurt him, so deeply scarred him. On the other hand he hated it because he knew it was false and ephemeral." In the same program biographer David Thomson relates, "Many, many people who worked with him, and came to work with him with the best intentions, went away in despair saying he's a spoiled kid. It has to be done his way or he goes away with some vast story about how he was wronged, he was offended, and I think that fits with the psychological pattern that he was a wronged kid."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="600" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/220px-Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/330px-Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/440px-Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Brando with <a href="/wiki/James_Baldwin" title="James Baldwin">James Baldwin</a> at the 1963 <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">Civil Rights March on Washington, D.C.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="1804" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/220px-Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/330px-Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg/440px-Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heston_Baldwin_Brando_Civil_Rights_March_1963.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
With <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Baldwin" title="James Baldwin">James Baldwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Poitier" title="Sidney Poitier">Sidney Poitier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Belafonte" title="Harry Belafonte">Harry Belafonte</a> at the March on Washington in 1963</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1946, Brando performed in <a href="/wiki/Ben_Hecht" title="Ben Hecht">Ben Hecht</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zionist" title="Zionist">Zionist</a> play <i><a href="/wiki/A_Flag_is_Born" title="A Flag is Born">A Flag is Born</a></i>. He attended some fundraisers for <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1960" title="United States presidential election, 1960">1960 presidential election</a>. In August 1963, he participated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/March_on_Washington" title="March on Washington">March on Washington</a> along with fellow celebrities <a href="/wiki/Harry_Belafonte" title="Harry Belafonte">Harry Belafonte</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Garner" title="James Garner">James Garner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burt_Lancaster" title="Burt Lancaster">Burt Lancaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Poitier" title="Sidney Poitier">Sidney Poitier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Along with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Newman" title="Paul Newman">Paul Newman</a>, Brando also participated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_rides" title="Freedom rides">freedom rides</a>.</p>
<p>In the aftermath of the 1968 <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.</a>, Brando made one of the strongest commitments to furthering King's work. Shortly after King's death, he announced that he was bowing out of the lead role of a major film (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Arrangement_(1969_film)" title="The Arrangement (1969 film)">The Arrangement</a></i>) (1969) which was about to begin production in order to devote himself to the civil rights movement. "I felt I'd better go find out where it is; what it is to be black in this country; what this rage is all about," Brando said on the late-night <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC-TV</a> talk show <i><a href="/wiki/The_Joey_Bishop_Show_(talk_show)" title="The Joey Bishop Show (talk show)">Joey Bishop Show</a></i>. In A&amp;E's <i>Biography</i> episode on Brando actor and co-star <a href="/wiki/Martin_Sheen" title="Martin Sheen">Martin Sheen</a> states, "I'll never forget the night that Reverend King was shot and I turned on the news and Marlon was walking through Harlem with <a href="/wiki/John_Lindsay" title="John Lindsay">Mayor Lindsay</a>. And there were snipers and there was a lot of unrest and he kept walking and talking through those neighborhoods with Mayor Lindsay. It was one of the most incredible acts of courage I ever saw, and it meant a lot and did a lot."</p>
<p>Brando's participation in the African-American civil rights movement actually began well before King's death. In the early 1960s, he contributed thousands of dollars to both the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">Southern Christian Leadership Conference</a> (S.C.L.C.) and to a scholarship fund established for the children of slain <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N.A.A.C.P." title="N.A.A.C.P.">N.A.A.C.P.</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Medgar_Evers" title="Medgar Evers">Medgar Evers</a>. By this time, Brando was already involved in films that carried messages about human rights: <i>Sayonara</i>, which addressed interracial romance, and <i>The Ugly American</i>, depicting the conduct of U.S. officials abroad and the deleterious effect on the citizens of foreign countries. For a time, he was also donating money to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Panther_Party" title="Black Panther Party">Black Panther Party</a> and considered himself a friend of founder <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Seale" title="Bobby Seale">Bobby Seale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> Brando ended his financial support for the group over his perception of its increasing radicalization, specifically a passage in a Panther pamphlet put out by <a href="/wiki/Eldridge_Cleaver" title="Eldridge Cleaver">Eldridge Cleaver</a> advocating indiscriminate violence, "for the Revolution."</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/45th_Academy_Awards" title="45th Academy Awards">1973 Academy Awards</a> ceremony, Brando refused to accept the Oscar for his performance in <i>The Godfather</i>. <a href="/wiki/Sacheen_Littlefeather" title="Sacheen Littlefeather">Sacheen Littlefeather</a> represented him at the ceremony. She appeared in full Apache attire and stated that owing to the "poor treatment of Native Americans in the film industry", Brando would not accept the award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> This occurred while the <a href="/wiki/Wounded_Knee_incident" title="Wounded Knee incident">standoff at Wounded Knee</a> was ongoing. The event grabbed the attention of the US and the world media. This was considered a major event and victory for the movement by its supporters and participants.</p>
<p>Outside of his film work, Brando appeared before the <a href="/wiki/California_State_Assembly" title="California State Assembly">California Assembly</a> in support of a fair housing law and personally joined picket lines in demonstrations protesting discrimination in housing developments.</p>
<p>He was also an activist against <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> In 1964, he favored a <a href="/wiki/Disinvestment_from_South_Africa" title="Disinvestment from South Africa">boycott</a> of his films in South Africa to prevent them from being shown to a segregated audience. He took part at a 1975 protest rally against American investments in South Africa and for the release of Nelson Mandela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> In 1989, Brando also starred in the film <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dry_White_Season" title="A Dry White Season">A Dry White Season</a></i>, based upon <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Brink" title="André Brink">André Brink</a>'s novel of the same name.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comments_on_Jews.2C_Hollywood_and_Israel">Comments on Jews, Hollywood and Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Comments on Jews, Hollywood and Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In an interview in <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> magazine in January 1979, Brando said: "You've seen every single race besmirched, but you never saw an image of the <a href="/wiki/Kike" title="Kike">kike</a> because the Jews were ever so watchful for that—and rightly so. They never allowed it to be shown on screen. The Jews have done so much for the world that, I suppose, you get extra disappointed because they didn't pay attention to that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> Brando made a similar comment on <i><a href="/wiki/Larry_King_Live" title="Larry King Live">Larry King Live</a></i> in April 1996, saying "<a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a> is run by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jews</a>; it is owned by Jews, and they should have a greater sensitivity about the issue of—of people who are suffering. Because they've exploited—we have seen the—we have seen the <a href="/wiki/Nigger" title="Nigger">nigger</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greaseball" title="Greaseball">greaseball</a>, we've seen the <a href="/wiki/Chink" title="Chink">chink</a>, we've seen the slit-eyed dangerous <a href="/wiki/Jap" title="Jap">Jap</a>, we have seen the wily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filipino_people" title="Filipino people">Filipino</a>, we've seen everything, but we never saw the kike. Because they knew perfectly well, that that is where you draw the wagons around." <a href="/wiki/Larry_King" title="Larry King">Larry King</a>, who is Jewish, replied, "When you say—when you say something like that, you are playing right in, though, to <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">anti-Semitic</a> people who say the Jews are—" Brando interrupted: "No, no, because I will be the first one who will appraise the Jews honestly and say 'Thank God for the Jews'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> Jay Kanter, Brando's agent, producer, and friend, defended him in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Variety" title="Daily Variety">Daily Variety</a></i>: "Marlon has spoken to me for hours about his fondness for the Jewish people, and he is a well-known supporter of Israel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JTA_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-JTA-96">[94]</a></sup> Similarly, Louie Kemp, in his article for <i>Jewish Journal</i>, wrote: "You might remember him as Don Vito Corleone, Stanley Kowalski or the eerie Col. Walter E. Kurtz in 'Apocalypse Now', but I remember Marlon Brando as a <a href="/wiki/Mensch" title="Mensch">mensch</a> and a personal friend of the Jewish people when they needed it most."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journal_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-journal-22">[20]</a></sup> In an interview with <i>NBC Today</i> one day after Brando's death, King also defended Brando's comments, saying that they had been blown out of proportion and taken out of context. In his later years, he became a vocal critic of Israel and a supporter of the Palestinian cause<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"That will be Brando's legacy whether he likes it or not—the stunning actor who embodied a poetry of anxiety that touched the deepest dynamics of his time and place."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Jack_Kroll" title="Jack Kroll">Jack Kroll</a> in 1994</cite></div>
<p>Brando was one of the most respected actors of the post-war era. He is listed by the American Film Institute as the fourth greatest male star whose screen debut occurred before or during 1950 (it occurred in 1950). He earned respect among critics for his memorable performances and charismatic screen presence. His greatest contribution was helping to popularize Method acting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> He is regarded as one of the greatest cinema actors of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flix_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-flix-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopedia Britannica</a></i> describes him as "the most celebrated of the method actors, and his slurred, mumbling delivery marked his rejection of classical dramatic training. His true and passionate performances proved him one of the greatest actors of his generation". It also notes the apparent paradox of his talent: "He is regarded as the most influential actor of his generation, yet his open disdain for the acting profession... often manifested itself in the form of questionable choices and uninspired performances. Nevertheless, he remains a riveting screen presence with a vast emotional range and an endless array of compulsively watchable idiosyncrasies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influence">Cultural influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cultural influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>He was our angry young man--the delinquent, the tough, the rebel--who stood at the center of our common experience.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a class="external text" href="http://www.theatlantic.com/past/docs/unbound/aandc/movies/movies4.htm" rel="nofollow">Marlon Brando: An American Hero</a></cite></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_(Madame_Tussaud).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG/170px-Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG/255px-Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG/340px-Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_%28Madame_Tussaud%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marlon_Brando_-_The_Wild_One_(Madame_Tussaud).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Madame_Tussauds" title="Madame Tussauds">Madame Tussauds</a> waxwork exhibit of Brando in <i>The Wild One</i> albeit with a later 1957/8 model <a href="/wiki/Triumph_Thunderbird" title="Triumph Thunderbird">Triumph Thunderbird</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marlon Brando is a cultural icon whose popularity has endured for over six decades. His rise to national attention in the 1950s had a profound effect on American culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-american-102">[100]</a></sup> According to film critic Pauline Kael, "Brando represented a reaction against the post-war mania for security. As a protagonist, the Brando of the early fifties had no code, only his instincts. He was a development from the gangster leader and the outlaw. He was antisocial because he knew society was crap; he was a hero to youth because he was strong enough not to take the crap ... Brando represented a contemporary version of the free American ... Brando is still the most exciting American actor on the screen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-american-102">[100]</a></sup> Sociologist Dr. Suzanne Mcdonald-Walker states: "Marlon Brando, sporting leather jacket, jeans, and moody glare, became a cultural icon summing up 'the road' in all its maverick glory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> His portrayal of the gang leader Johnny Strabler in <i>The Wild One</i> has become an iconic image, used both as a symbol of rebelliousness and a fashion accessory that includes a Perfecto style motorcycle jacket, a tilted cap, jeans and sunglasses. Johnny's haircut inspired a craze for sideburns, followed by <a href="/wiki/James_Dean" title="James Dean">James Dean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomer_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomer-104">[102]</a></sup> Dean copied Brando's acting style extensively and Presley used Brando's image as a model for his role in <i><a href="/wiki/Jailhouse_Rock_(film)" title="Jailhouse Rock (film)">Jailhouse Rock</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> The "I coulda been a contender" scene from <i>On the Waterfront</i>, according to the author of <i>Brooklyn Boomer</i>, Martin H. Levinson, is "one of the most famous scenes in motion picture history, and the line itself has become part of America's cultural lexicon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomer_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-bloomer-104">[102]</a></sup> Brando's powerful "Wild One" image was still, as of 2011, being marketed by the makers of his <a href="/wiki/Triumph_Thunderbird" title="Triumph Thunderbird">Triumph Thunderbird</a> motorcycle, in a range of clothing inspired by his character from the film and licensed by Brando's estate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brando was also considered a male sex symbol. <a href="/wiki/Linda_Williams_(film_scholar)" title="Linda Williams (film scholar)">Linda Williams</a> writes: "Marlon Brando [was] the quintessential American male sex symbol of the late fifties and early sixties".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_acting">Views on acting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Views on acting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Brando was notoriously reluctant to discuss acting in interviews,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but many of his insights are on record. In his autobiography <i>Songs My Mother Taught Me</i>, Brando observed:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I've always thought that one benefit of acting is that it gives actors a chance to express feelings that they are normally unable to vent in real life. Intense emotions buried inside you can come smoking out the back of your head, and I suppose in terms of psychodrama this can be helpful. In hindsight, I guess my emotional insecurity as a child—the frustrations of not being allowed to be who I was, of wanting love and not being able to get it, of realizing that I was of no value—may have helped me as an actor, at least in a small way. It probably gave me a certain intensity that most people don't have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (December 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>He also confessed that, while having great admiration for the theater, he did not return to it after his initial success primarily because the work left him drained emotionally:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What I remember most about <i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i> was the emotional grind of acting in it six nights and two afternoons. Try to imagine what it was like walking on stage at 8:30 every night having to yell, scream, cry, break dishes, kick the furniture, punch the walls and <i>experience</i> the same intense, wrenching emotions night after night, trying each time to evoke in audiences the same emotions I felt. It was exhausting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (December 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Brando repeatedly credited Stella Adler and her understanding of the Stanislavsky acting technique for bringing realism to American cinema, but also added:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This school of acting served the American theater and motion pictures well, but it was restricting. The American theater has never been able to present Shakespeare or classical drama of any kind satisfactorily. We simply do not have the style, the regard for the language or the cultural disposition ... You cannot mumble in Shakespeare. You cannot improvise, and you are required to adhere strictly to the text. The English theater has a sense of language that we do not recognize ... In the United States the English language has developed almost into a patois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandoLindsey1994-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (December 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the 2015 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Listen_to_Me_Marlon" title="Listen to Me Marlon">Listen to Me Marlon</a></i>, Brando shared his thoughts on playing a death scene, stating, "That's a tough scene to play. You have to make 'em believe that you are dying ... Try to think of the most intimate moment you've ever had in your life."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Brando's favorite actors were <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Tracy" title="Spencer Tracy">Spencer Tracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Barrymore" title="John Barrymore">John Barrymore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fredric_March" title="Fredric March">Fredric March</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">James Cagney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Muni" title="Paul Muni">Paul Muni</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_legacy">Financial legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Financial legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon his death in 2004, Brando left an estate valued at $21.6 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> Brando's estate still earned about $9 million in 2005, the year following his death, according to <i>Forbes</i>. That year Brando was named one of the top-earning deceased celebrities in the world by the magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filmography">Filmography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Filmography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Brando_filmography" title="Marlon Brando filmography">Marlon Brando filmography</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marlon_Brando&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_Marlon_Brando" title="List of awards and nominations received by Marlon Brando">List of awards and nominations received by Marlon Brando</a></div>
<p>Brando was named the fourth <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Stars" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Stars">greatest male star</a> whose screen debut occurred before or during 1950 by the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> and part of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TIME" title="TIME">TIME</a></i> magazine's <a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> He was also named one of the top 10 "Icons of the Century" by <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flix_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-flix-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup></p>
